10.1016/j.jmp.2010.06.002
Theories of extensive measurement usually assume an “Archimedean axiom”, designed to exclude the possibility of infinite or infinitesimal differences among the objects of measurement. The Archimedean axiom is necessary for a standard, real-valued representation that is additive with respect to concatenation. In this paper, a generalized theory of extensive measurement is developed, which allows infinite and infinitesimal differences. The representation is in terms of ordered  
                         n 
                      -tuples of ordered pairs of real numbers. Such structures will be labeled “lexical vectors”.

The results of the paper are entirely general, and a detailed discussion of possible applications must await another occasion. It is not difficult, however, to think of contexts where the Archimedean axiom may fail to hold. Among moral philosophers, it has for a long time been a fairly common view that certain values are lexicographically superior to others. Classical examples are Francis Hutcheson in the early 18th century, and John Stuart Mill in the mid-19th century ( Hutcheson, 1968; Mill, 1998 ). In the early 20th century, Franz Brentano judged it “quite possible for there to be a class of goods which could be increased  ad indefinitum  but without exceeding a given finite good” ( Brentano, 1969 ). Similarly, W.D. Ross found it plausible that, although virtue and pleasure are both good, “ no  amount of pleasure is equal to any amount of virtue, that in fact virtue belongs to a higher order of value, beginning at a point higher on the scale of value than pleasure ever reaches …” ( Ross, 1930 , p. 150). 
                         1 
                      
                      
                         1 
                         These quotations from Brentano and Ross, as well as a list of other philosophers making similar claims, can be found in  Arrhenius, 2005 , p. 97.

Applications are also likely to be found in more psychologically oriented value and preference research. Thus, value orders with a lexicographic structure are investigated in research on taboo tradeoffs and protected values. Empirical studies show that people tend to regard certain value tradeoffs, involving for example acts of friendship or the preservation of species versus monetary gain, as prohibited, and often morally objectionable even to consider. See  Baron & Spranca, 1997; Fiske & Tetlock, 1997 . 
                         2 
                      
                      
                         2 
                         An anonymous referee of this journal provided these references. 
                       Lexicographic preference orders not involving protected values seem very likely, as well. Even if a person attaches some positive value to a paper clip, there is probably no number  
                         n 
                      , such that she prefers owning  
                         n 
                       paper clips to living a long, happy life. (To make this example maximally convincing, we may assume that she would not be able to sell the paper clips.) Or, as John S. Chipman rhetorically asks, “if you are a ‘security risk’, is there some level of competence and intelligence high enough to induce the State Department to employ you nevertheless?” ( Chipman, 1960 , p. 221). Putative examples are easily multiplied. See, e.g.,  Thrall, 1954 , p. 185;  Chipman, 1960 , p. 221;  Roberts, 2009 , p. 126.

While such preference or value structures cannot be given an additive real-valued representation, they are perfectly amenable to the kind of representation we shall propose. This is not to suggest, of course, that the Archimedean axiom is the only obstacle to applying extensive measurement directly to preference structures. There are other well-known problems. In particular, even if a suitable operation of concatenation can be defined, the monotonicity axiom ( Axiom G3  in our theory) is often questionable. See, e.g.,  Krantz, Luce, Suppes, & Tversky, 1971 , p. 124;  Roberts, 2009 , p. 125f. Since particular applications are not the focus of this paper, however, these issues will not be discussed here. For present purposes, we shall proceed on the assumption that the structures to be investigated are of some theoretical interest.

The idea of formulating a generalized, non-Archimedean version of extensive measurement is not entirely new. For related previous results, see in particular  Narens, 1974, 1985 , ch. 5, and  Skala, 1975 , ch. 4. The approach of these authors is, however, rather different from ours. They use sophisticated mathematical tools, such as nonstandard real numbers and ultrapower techniques, and their results are presented in a highly abstract fashion. (Since a useful summary of these results would require citing a rather large number of background definitions, no such summary will be given here. Instead, the reader is referred to the works cited.) Moreover, these works are primarily concerned with representation theorems, whereas uniqueness problems for non-Archimedean structures are hardly discussed at all. Theories of non-Archimedean difference measurement, as distinguished from extensive measurement, are developed by  Chipman (1960, 1971) ,  Hausner (1954) ,  Richter (1971)  and  Thrall (1954) . These theories, too, tend to be mathematically advanced, and are presented at a high level of abstraction.

In contrast, the theory to be developed here employs only simple and familiar mathematical concepts. Furthermore, our main result,  Theorem 2  in the next section, is fully analogous to the standard theorem of extensive measurement (presented as  Theorem 1  below). Thus, it states straightforward representation and uniqueness conditions, and covers structures with both positive and negative objects.

Let us start by giving a brief outline of our theory. First, the axioms of a standard closed extensive structure are rehearsed. Then the concept of a lexical vector is introduced, and basic mathematical relations and operations are defined for such structures. This allows us to succinctly formulate the representation and uniqueness problem we want to solve. After stating four of our theory’s seven axioms, a “rank” relation is defined, with the intuitive import that two objects are of the same rank if neither is lexicographically superior to the other. Further, an object is said to be “rank uniform” if it is not a concatenation of any two objects of different rank. The remaining three axioms are then introduced, and we show that every object is a finite concatenation of rank uniform objects. In our representation, every rank uniform object is assigned an ordered pair of real numbers. The first number in such a pair represents the rank of the object in question, while the second number represents the object’s value, as measured within its rank. Given our axioms, it is shown that the axioms of a standard, closed extensive structure hold for the rank uniform objects within each rank. Hence, an object’s intra-rank value is measured on a ratio scale. If  
                         a 
                       and  
                         b 
                       have different rank, the concatenated object  
                         a 
                         ∘ 
                         b 
                       is assigned an ordered pair of two ordered pairs of reals; one pair representing the value of  
                         a 
                      , the other representing the value of  
                         b 
                      . More generally, a concatenation of  
                         n 
                       rank uniform objects, each of different rank, is assigned a lexical vector; i.e., an ordered  
                         n 
                      -tuple of ordered pairs of reals. Finally, a generalized form of similarity transformation is defined, and it is shown that the theory yields what is essentially a ratio scale.

Axiom G5  is necessary only if  
                         X 
                       contains both positive and negative objects. Given  Axioms G1–G4 , it is always satisfied unless one of  
                         a 
                       and  
                         b 
                       is positive, and the other is negative. Its purpose is, roughly, to ensure that the operation  
                         ∘ 
                       is closed in each  
                         
                            
                               ≈ 
                            
                            
                               U 
                            
                         
                      -equivalence class (see  Proposition 5  below). This is necessary for every  
                         
                            
                               ≈ 
                            
                            
                               U 
                            
                         
                      -equivalence class to be a closed extensive structure. The rationale of  Axiom G6  is similar. It implies that the Archimedean  Axiom E4  holds within each  
                         
                            
                               ≈ 
                            
                            
                               U 
                            
                         
                      -equivalence class (see  Proposition 4  below).  Axiom G7 , finally, is needed primarily in order to make sure that every object is a finite concatenation of rank uniform objects (see  Proposition 7  below).

The plausibility of these axioms may of course depend on the particular area of application. A potential objection to  Axiom G5  is that it introduces a kind of Archimedean assumption. Since the axiom only applies to  
                         
                            
                               ≈ 
                            
                            
                               U 
                            
                         
                      -equivalent objects and their concatenations, however, this Archimedean assumption is very weak and restricted. Intuitively,  
                         
                            
                               ≈ 
                            
                            
                               U 
                            
                         
                      -equivalent objects are Archimedean equivalents anyway.  Axiom G5  hence only adds a condition about how positive and negative Archimedean equivalents are related to each other.  Axiom G7  does not seem to impose a severe restriction, either. In most applications the number of ranks will probably be finite, whereas  Axiom G7  allows for a countable infinity of ranks.

That  Axioms G1–G7  are mutually consistent follows from the fact that, letting  
                         I 
                       denote the integers, they are all satisfied by the structure  
                         
                            ( 
                            I 
                            , 
                            ≥ 
                            , 
                            + 
                            ) 
                         
                      , albeit vacuously in the case of  Axiom G7 . We leave it to the reader to verify that the axioms are logically independent; i.e., none of them can be derived from the others.

In this section we shall consider two potential objections to our theory. First, lexical vectors might seem unnecessarily complex, as means of representing the lexicographic structures we are investigating. Will not single ordered pairs of reals do equally well? Now to the second potential objection. It may be questioned whether  Theorem 2  suffices to show that

                         f 
                      -values, representing rank, are unique only up to order preserving, i.e., monotone increasing transformations.

                               b
